This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission between June 26 and July 12,
1995. Members of the appraisal mission included Messrs/Mmes: Kristin Gilbertson (Task
Manager), Alan Armstrong-Wright (Urban Transport Specialist), Kari Homanen
(Environmental Specialist), Martin Gambrill (Civil Engineer), Hortensia Hill (Procurement
Specialist), Betsy McGean (Social Ecologist), and Gulam Dhalla (Financial Analyst). The
following representatives of the donor agencies and co-financiers also participated in the
mission: Lars Eklund and Marianne Tegman (BITS), Michael Jensen (Danish Environmental
Protection Agency) and Leo Langgaard (EU PHARE). Technical assistance for project

Gouarne (EMTIE), Andrew Hamer (EA2EU) and Felix Jakob (ECIIN). The Division Chief

REPUBLIC OF LATVIA
MUNICIPAL SERVICES DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:              Republic of Latvia
Implementing Agencies:  The City of Riga
The Daugavpils Municipal Enterprise Udensvads
Municipal Development Fund of the Public Investment Program
Poverty Category:      Not applicable.
IBRD Loan Amount:      US $ 27.3 million equivalent
Terns:                 Seventeen years, including four years of grace, at the Bank's standard
variable interest rate and commitment fees.
Commitment Fee:        0.75 % on undisbursed loan balances, beginning 60 days after signing, less
any waiver.
On-Lending Terms:      To Riga City and to the Daugavpils Municipal Enterprise Udensvads with
municipal guarantee in US$ at the IBRD interest rate plus a mark-up for
cross-currency risk and loan administration (up to 30% of the Bank
interest rate), to be repaid over seventeen years including four years of
grace.
To beneficiaries of the Municipal Development Fund in US$ at the IBRD
interest rate plus a mark-up for cross-currency risk and loan
administration to be repaid over a period of up to seventeen years
including up to four years of grace.
Project Objectives:    The project aims to support the extensive decentralization and
intergovernmental fiscal reforms already instituted by the government by
providing access to investment financing and technical assistance needed
for rehabilitation and improved management of municipal services. The
project's specific objectives are to: (i) improve efficiency and quality of
public transport services in Riga, improve the financial condition and
promote further corporatization of the three Riga public transport
companies; (ii) improve the quality of drinking water in Daugavpils,
reduce discharge of untreated and partially treated wastewaters to the
Daugava River and Baltic Sea, improve the financial management and
promote further corporatization of the Daugavpils water utility; and (iii)
pursue similar objectives in other communities through financing of the
Municipal Development Fund (MDF).



- ii -
Project Description:      The project would finance: (i) rehabilitation of the Riga urban transport
system through provision of priority spares, replacement and repair of
dilapidated vehicles; introduction of improved financial and operational
management systems; strengthening of regulatory capability and further
corporatization of transport enterprises; (ii) construction of a new
groundwater supply system in Daugavpils, metering, addition of biological
treatment capacity to the existing sewage works, rehabilitation of pumping
stations, implementation of improved billing and financial systems,
improved financial and operational performance and further
corporatization of the utility; (iii) small scale investment projects in other
communities throughout Latvia by a Municipal Development Fund (MDF)
to be established in the Public Investment Program; and (iv) technical
assistance to support project implementation and agreed policy and
organizational reforms.
Project Benefits:         The project supports development of municipal infrastructure, identified as
a priority in the Bank's Country Assistance Strategy and the Government's
recent Public Investment Program. The project benefits include: (i)
increased reliability, comfort and efficiency of public transport systems in
Riga, improved financial viability of public transport companies, a
reduced rate of increase in automobile traffic and reduction in the likely
rate of increase in air pollution; (ii) improved quality and safety of
drinking water supply in Daugavpils, decreased operating costs,
introduction of demand management and a reduction in the pollution of
the Daugava River, Riga Bay, and Baltic Sea; (iii) similar benefits in other
communities obtaining financing for similar projects through the MDF;
and (iv) establishment of mechanisms that can be used in the medium term
for financing future municipal services projects and which can form the
basis for future efforts to channel funds to the sector.
Project Risks:            Major risks are that: (i) participating municipalities would not adhere to
agreed policy conditionality and/or financial and performance covenants
including provision of counterpart financing; (ii) inability of local
governments to manage project implementation leading to construction and
procurement delays; (iii) inability of local governments to prepare
satisfactory investment proposals for financing under the MDF; and (iv)
inadequate supervision of MDF projects.
These risks will be mitigated by: (i) prior agreement with municipalities
on terms and conditions required for sub-loan effectiveness and, in
particular, related to financial covenants, organizational arrangements and
local counterpart financing; (ii) a requirement that sub-loan agreements
between the participating municipalities and the Ministry of Finance on
behalf of the Government of Latvia would enable the Ministry of Finance
to deduct interest and principal repayments from taxes and other revenues
collected on behalf of local governments by the state tax administration;
(iii) provision of technical assistance for critical areas of project
implementation; (iv) adequate staffing of the PMUs for the three primary



- iii -
sub-projects; (v) addition of consultant staff to the PIP unit to assist with
administration of the MDF and to support municipalities in preparation
and supervision of projects; and (vi) establishment of a central project
coordination unit to monitor project implementation.
Environmental Aspects:   An environmental review for all identified investment components has
been prepared consistent with the provisions of the World Bank's
Operational Directive 4.01 on "Environmental Assessment" and in
accordance with the laws and procedures of the Government of Latvia.
Appropriate environmental review will also be required of projects to be
financed under the MDF component of the project.
The project has a B rating since most of the potential impacts are expected
to be beneficial. Support of urban transport in Riga will reduce fuel
consumption and lessen air pollution through replacement and
rehabilitation of older vehicles and by increasing attractiveness of public
transport as compared to private automobiles. Development of new
groundwater supplies in Daugavpils will increase the safety, quality and
reliability of drinking water supplies. Addition of biological treatment
facilities at the existing sewage treatment works in Daugavpils and
conservation of water will reduce pollution of the Daugava River, Riga
Bay and Baltic Sea. Each of these activities will be monitored and
evaluated for environmental impact throughout the life of the project.
Costs and Financing:    Total project cost is estimated at US$45.4 million of which: (i) US$27.3
million equivalent would be financed by the Bank; (ii) US$2.0 million
would be financed by NEFCO; (iii) US$9.0 million by grants from EU
PHARE and the Governments of Sweden, Denmark and Finland; and (iv)
US$7. 1 million in local counterpart financing.
Rate of Return:         The economic rate of return (ERR) for the Riga urban transport
component is estimated at 22%. This represents US$18.7 million or 41%
of total project costs. The benefits for the Daugavpils water and
wastewater component are not quantifiable, although assumed to be
significant given the positive impact on health and enviromnent; least cost
analysis has been used in determining the appropriate investment program.
Sub-projects to be financed under the MDF will be required to
demonstrate an economic rate of return of 12%.
Participation:          Preparation of the project has been carried out in close cooperation with
the Ministry of Environmental Protection and Regional Development, the
City of Riga, the Riga public transport companies, the City of Daugavpils,
the Daugavpils Municipal Enterprise Udensvads, the Public Investment
Program unit, Ministry of Finance and the Union of Local Governments.
Map:                    IBRD Map No. 27295
Project ID:             LV-PA-34584






LATVIA
MUNICIPAL SERVICES DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Country and Sector Background
1.1           Latvia is a country of 2.6 million people with an area of 25,000 square miles (64,600
square kilometers). It is situated on the eastern coast of the Baltic Sea and is bounded by Estonia to
the north, Lithuania to the south, Russia and Belarus to the east and has a 500-kilometer coastline on
the Baltic Sea.
1.2          Since the restoration of Latvian independence in 1991, the country has undertaken a
program of political and economic reforms aimed at achieving a transformation to a market economy.
A formal macroeconomic stabilization program was launched in July 1992, supported by an IMF
standby arrangement. A transitional currency, the Latvian ruble, was introduced in 1992 and was
replaced by the permanent currency, the Lats, in May 1993. The Government has been successful in
containing monetary expansion and fiscal deficits, though the Government has recently been faced
with an unanticipated revenue shortfall for 1995. Initial inflation has been brought under control,
falling from over 900 percent in 1992 to 36 percent in 1994. It is projected to decline to
approximately 25 percent for 1995. The sharp decline in GDP which occurred during the initial
adjustment period has been arrested and initial indicators show positive growth in 1995.
1.3          Structural adjustment in key areas has progressed significantly: (i) most prices have
been liberalized; (ii) an open trade regime has been introduced and privatization of small businesses,
agricultural land and banking institutions is well advanced and housing privatization has recently
commenced; and (iii) the Government is reforming the social safety net through better means-testing
to target benefits to low-income groups. Banking and industrial sector reforms, however, have been
slower than initially anticipated. A banking crisis has emerged over the spring of 1995 and will pose
additional demands on Government resources to fund planned partial compensation of depositors.
National elections took place in early October 1995. Since no party woIn a commanding lead, another
coalition government will be created. While the implications for the continuation of the economic
reform process are not yet clear, there are good indications that major political parties are committed
to the reform.
1.4          The Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) was discussed by the Board in September
1994. The World Bank's overall objective in Latvia has been shifting from supporting economic
stabilization and early structural policy measures to deepening structural reforms and promoting
private and public sector investments. In doing so, the Bank will also support decentralization efforts
made by the government, in particular, in delegating authority to municipalities in administrative and
financial decision making. The focus of the Bank's strategy is to: (i) enhance private sector
development; (ii) improve public infrastructure services and energy conservation; (iii) strengthen the
social safety net and improve social services; and (iv) address pressing environment concerns.
Investments in municipal infrastructure and environment have been identified as a priority in the
Public Investment Program (1995-1997) of the Government; they are also in line with the CAS
objectives and will be supported by the proposed project.



- 2 -
1.5    Although basic coverage by municipal services is relatively high, substantial investment is
required for rehabilitation and improvement of existing facilities, many of which are in extremely
poor condition given the lack of maintenance and investment during the adjustment period following
re-independence and given the already poor state of much plant and equipment. Urban transport
systems are operating at crush capacity given low vehicle availability, aging fleets and lack of spares
and new replacement vehicles. Municipal dump sites are nearing capacity and continued operations
pose a threat to groundwater supplies in many areas. Water quality is poor, distribution systems
inefficient and wastewater treatment limited. District heating systems are inefficient which leads to
higher costs and increased burden on local inhabitants. Roads are in poor condition and continue to
deteriorate.
1.6           In the present fiscal environment, neither state nor local governments have sufficient
resources to undertake needed investments out of current revenues, and public investment of all kinds
has been severely constrained during the adjustment period. At the same time, the borrowing
capacity of local governments and associated municipal enterprises has increased substantially due to
reform of intergovernmental fiscal relations combined with substantial increases in user charges and
continued movement towards cost recovery.
B. Local Government in Latvia
1.7            Since re-independence, Latvia has embarked on an ambitious program of
decentralization, reversing the decades old tendency towards centralization of authority during the
Soviet period and restoring the more traditional government structures which prevailed during the
inter-war years. Local governments have become increasingly important and now account for almost
one half of total public sector spending. They have complete responsibility for the delivery of critical
municipal services including water supply, sewage treatment, solid waste management, urban
transport, district heating and maintenance of urban roads. Local governments are also responsible
for housing and housing maintenance and share responsibility with the central government for health,
education and social services.
1.8            There are two levels of local government in Latvia. The first level consists of the
rural municipalities or pagasts (493) and the town municipalities (69). The second level consists of
the rajon (district or county) governments (26) and the seven republican cities which have rajon status
but also perform the functions of municipalities.' First-level local governments are responsible for
municipal services, housing, primary health care facilities and some social assistance. Second-level
local governments are responsible for public transport, industrial waste management, clinics, tertiary
care facilities, old peoples homes and homeless shelters. Responsibility for primary and secondary
education is shared by the two levels of local government, and teacher salaries are paid by the central
governments. Republican cities perform the functions of both levels of government. These
responsibilities and the structure of local government are laid out in the Law on Local Government
(June 1994).
1.9            Local governments are democratically elected. Two rounds of elections have been
held since re-independence, the last being in June 1994. Local government officials are subject to the
same civil service requirements as the central government and a massive education and skills
'The seven republican cities are Riga, Daugavpils, Jelgava, Jurmala, Liepaja, Rezekne and Ventspils.
.1- -                  W   t [ }a UL  v  WtJll A  ULz§11 l11BA 1i 1§11



development program  has been launched to enable local leaders to meet their substantially increased
responsibilities. The program has been led by the Ministry of State Reforms with assistance from the
Union of Local Government Training Center and the Danish Local Government Association with
financial support from the EU PHARE program.
1.10          In contrast to many other countries in transition, the system of revenue assignment to
local governments is both transparent and objective thanks to a series of reforms in intergovernmental
fiscal relations adopted over the past two years. Latvia has successfully moved away from a system
of negotiated transfers to a pure tax assignment model. Local goveinments have been assigned 100%
of the revenues from the personal income, land and property taxes and also receive a portion of the
natural resources and forest taxes. Unlike many other countries, the land and property taxes are
relatively unimportant sources of revenue. The income tax is the major source of local government
revenue. In all cases, the tax rates and the tax base are determined by the central government with
little opportunity for local discretion. Given the large income disparity among different regions in
Latvia and between rural and urban areas, there is partial equalization of revenues to ensure adequate
funding for minimal service provision in basic services such as health and education.
1.11          Local government budgets are formed independently according to laws and regulations
established by the central government. Accounting and reporting requirements are clearly specified in
the Law on Local Budget and the Law on Budget. These laws also provide for central government
oversight of local government borrowing and guarantees to ensure prudent financial management until
local governments gain experience in this area.
C. Organization of Municipal Services
1.12          Municipal services (water supply, wastewater collection and treatment, solid waste
management, public transport and district heating) are delivered primarily by autonomous municipal
enterprises. These enterprises are wholly owned by the municipality but are separate and independent
budgetary institutions with independent legal status as provided under the Law on Municipal
Enterprises (1989). The legal status of municipal enterprises is similar in many respects to that of
joint stock companies. Municipal enterprises can hire and fire at will, have the right to establish their
own budgets independently, can borrow and enter into contracts. Though there is no actual board of
directors, the municipality as the owner of the enterprise appoints and reviews the performance of the
president. The municipality retains the right to determine user charges; however, it is required to
compensate the enterprise for losses incurred by failure of the municipality to raise user charges to
cost recovery levels.
1.13          During the Soviet period, user charges for municipal services were subsidized to an
extreme degree. These subsidy programs are no longer affordable and most municipalities have
already substantially increased user charges for municipal services. In Daugavpils, water and
sewerage charges increased 10 times between January 1992 and June 1994. In Riga, water and
sewerage charges increased 20 times during the same period. Similar changes have taken place in
district heating, urban transport and solid waste collection. In many cases, user charges have been
increased to a level where they cover basic operating costs; however, in virtually all cases charges do
not cover costs of maintenance, depreciation or investment. With the exception of public transport,
most municipal enterprises do not receive any subsidies at all. Continued real increases are still
required to achieve full cost recovery. And, once that has been reached, there still remains a need to
ensure periodic increases to compensate for still significant inflation.



- 4 -
1.14           Funding for maintenance and investment are further constrained by massive
accumulation of arrears for many services. Since all billings are counted as revenues and since no
allowance is made for bad or doubtful accounts, income for most enterprises is overstated and there is
substantial pressure on cash flow. In the water sector, the average days receivable can be as high as
200 to 300 as compared to a target of 30 days or less. There is some evidence of arrears from
household consumers, but the most significant problem with arrears is with industrial consumers. In
the case of Riga water, 96% of arrears are due to industry. In the case of Daugavpils water, 61 % of
arrears are due to a single company. Municipal authorities have hesitated to enforce collection and
are reluctant to disconnect non-paying or late-paying customers due to the potential political
ramifications and due to concerns that such disconnection would hasten closure of what in many cases
are already bankrupt enterprises.
1.15           The central government provides a small number of individual grants out of the state
budget to finance selected investment projects, mainly for wastewater treatment projects and school
construction. These funds are rather limited given the constrained resources of government at all
levels and are insufficient to fund even a small share of the necessary investments at the local level
(such grants amounted to only Ls 1.4 million in 1994). Local financial institutions are willing to lend
to local governments when such loans are secured by commercially attractive property; however, the
terms are too short and rates are too high to enable local governments to tap into these funds for
infrastructure investment.
D. Private Sector Involvement in Municipal Services
1.16           There is already some private sector involvement in the provision of municipal
services in Latvia. Solid waste collection services have been privatized in both Riga and Liepaja and
are now being provided by independent contractors. In Riga, private bus operators provide
approximately 5% of public transport services. No water or district heating enterprises have been
privatized or contracted out. In such cases, private sector investment is limited given the hestitation
of local governments to allow enterprises to charge cost recovery tariffs and guarantee an appropriate
return on assets.



- 5 -
II. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives
2.1           The project aims to support the extensive decentralization and intergovernmental fiscal
reforms already instituted by the government by providing access to investment financing and
technical assistance needed for rehabilitation and improved management of municipal services. The
project's specific objectives are to:
(a)    improve efficiency and quality of public transport services in Riga, improve the
financial condition and promote further corporatization of the three Riga public
transport companies;
(b)    improve the quality of drinking water in Daugavpils, reduce discharge of untreated
and partially treated wastewaters to the Daugava River and Baltic Sea, improve the
financial management and promote further corporatization of the Daugavpils water
utility; and
(c)    pursue similar objectives in other communities through financing of the Municipal
Development Fund.
B. Origin and Rationale
2.2          Investment in municipal infrastructure has been identified as a priority in the Public
Investment Program of the Latvian Government and in the Bank's Country Assistance Strategy. The
present project was identified in early 1994 after discussions with both central and local govermnent
officials regarding issues and priorities in the municipal sector. Three priority subprojects were
identified after discussions with state and local government officials based on their relative priority to
the local communities, relative priority as compared with other projects within the sector, and
perceived willingness of local communities to cooperate on a series of policy and operational reforms
to improve municipal enterprise performance. These are: (i) rehabilitation of Riga urban transport;
(ii) upgrading of the Daugavpils water and wastewater treatment systems; and (iii) establishment of a
pilot Municipal Development Fund (MDF). All three subprojects are included in the Public
Investment Program developed by the Government of Latvia which has been reviewed previously by
the Bank. Several priority subsectors were considered (including solid waste management
improvement in Riga but it was decided to consider this under a possible future project) and the three
were selected because of: (i) the willingness of the communities to undertake significant institutional
and pricing reforms in the subsectors; (ii) the critical importance of the two subsectors (urban
transport and water/wastewater) to, respectively, the capital (which contains more than a third of the
country's population) and the second largest city; and (iii) the potential for improved municipal
management in smaller communities through the pilot MDF.
2.3           Riga Urban Transport. Throughout Latvia, the quality of urban transport services has
seriously deteriorated given a lack of investment in maintenance and investment in the period since re-
independence. However, the situation is particularly critical in Riga which has a population of
approximately 860,000 and is the only major city in Latvia. Public transport, upon which 80 to 90%
of the Riga population are highly dependent, provides infrequent, crowded and unreliable service.
Many vehicles are idle due to the cost and difficulty of maintaining the aging fleets. Without



- 6 -
sufficient cash flow to begin retirement and replacement of aged vehicles, public transport services in
Riga will be severely compromised, vehicle operating costs will increase and reliability decrease as
vehicle breakdowns become increasingly frequent. This could hasten the trend toward greater use of
private cars to the detriment of city traffic conditions and the environment.
2.4           The majority of public transport services are provided by the three public transport
enterprises owned by the Riga City Council: bus services are provided by Imanta and Talava and
tram and trolleybus services by the Tram and Trolleybus Park (TTP). Since re-independence,
private operators have been free to apply to the municipality for licenses to run public transport
services in Riga. However, the number of private buses and minibuses in operation has never been
high, and in recent years has declined to less than 50 small "taxi buses." The lack of interest is
almost certainly due to poor returns arising from competition with the municipal enterprises which are
required to keep fares below full cost recovery levels and due to the high cost of credit. In the
medium-term the municipal enterprises should be privatized after liberalization of fares allows for
them to become financially viable and after strengthening the regulatory capacity of the existing Riga
Board of Municipal Services and Transportation (RBMST) to ensure a minimum level of competition,
safety and coordination of routes.
2.5           Daugavpils Water and Wastewater. Daugavpils is Latvia's second largest city with a
population of 120,000 people, the majority of wliich belong to Latvia's ethnic Russian minority. The
city grew rapidly after the second world war due to rapid industrialization. Presently, Daugavpils
derives most of its water supply from the Daugava River which is severely polluted by insufficiently
treated and untreated sewage water discharges upstream in Belarus and Russia. An incident involving
accidental dumping of cyanide into the water several years ago has made development of groundwater
resources a top priority. Drinking water does not meet Latvian or EU standards for iron, manganese,
aluminum or trihalomethanes (THMs), which are a by-product of the chlorination of raw water with a
high concentration of organics. While the iron and other metals are mainly an aesthetic concern, the
THMs are carcinogenic compounds. Although the relationship between cancer and THMs in drinking
water is difficult to prove, the general tendency is to move away from using contaminated or
otherwise high organic content surface waters for drinking water supply and, instead use groundwater
or treat water with activated carbon filters and ozonation.
2.6           The wastewater treatment works are limited to mechanical treatment and do not have
a biological treatment phase. Daugavpils has been identified, together with Riga and Liepaja, as one
of the three "hotspots" in Latvia for the Baltic Sea Joint Comprehensive Environmental Action
Program prepared under the coordination of the Baltic Marine Environment Protection Commission
(HELCOM) based in Helsinki. This program was adopted by the Ministers of Environment of the
Baltic Sea region, including Latvia, in April 1992. The proposed rehabilitation of the Daugavpils
wastewater treatment works would reduce the discharge of untreated and partially treated sewage and
would have a positive environmental impact on nearby beaches and recreational areas, the River
Daugava, the Riga Bay and the Baltic Sea. Daugavpils is the only remaining "hotspot" in Latvia as
improvements in wastewater treatment are being made with the support of the Bank under the Liepaja
Environment Project and improvements in wastewater treatment for the City of Riga are being
contemplated with the support of the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EBRD).
2.7           There are no immediate plans for privatization given the poor financial performance
of the Daugavpils Municipal Enterprise Udensvads (DMEU); this is due mainly to failure of the
municipality to allow imposition of user charges sufficient for full cost recovery. Substantial



improvement in financial performance, depoliticization of user charge determination and an increase
in the regulatory capacity of the municipality should result from the project and should elicit an
increase in private sector interest and involvement over the medium term.
2.8           Establishment of a Pilot Municipal Development Fund. Although Riga and the larger
cities are able to access loan financing for rehabilitation of municipal infrastructure from international
financing agencies such as the World Bank, most municipalities are not of sufficient size to justify
direct involvement. Investment needs in these communities remain unfunded or receive support
directly from the state budget. Such funding from the state budget is extremely limited and there is
no local lending at rates and terms appropriate for financing infrastructure investment. Establishment
of a Municipal Development Fund will enable the Government to channel loan funds from
international financing agencies to communities unable to borrow directly and will reduce dependence
on grants from the state budget for investments with primarily local benefits.
2.9           Preparation of feasibility studies for the major project components was initiated with
the assistance of a Japanese Policy and Human Resources Development Grant, the EU PHARE, and
the Ministry of Economic Affairs of the Netherlands. At the same time, the Bank initiated a program
of formal and informal sector work in local government finance to assess the viability of the
municipal finance framework in Latvia and its impact on local government borrowing capacity and
development of municipal services. This has formed the focal point for an ongoing policy dialogue
with the government on these issues and has been supported by a longer-term program of assistance
by US Treasury representatives in Latvia and the Danish Association of Local Governments whose
activities in local government training and civil service reform are being sponsored under the EU
PHARE program.
C. Rationale for Bank Involvement
2.10         Involvement of the Bank will foster introduction of requisite policy reforms,
institutional development, financial and operational improvements needed to ensure sustainability of
project investments and creditworthiness of beneficiaries. Since investment financing of the
magnitude required is not available from other donors (except EBRD in the case of Riga water, see
para 2.6), Bank participation will provide critically needed investment financing and help mobilize
limited resources from other donors and financiers.
D. Lessons Learned from Past Operations
2.11          The Municipal Development Sector Review (R-94171 dated August 22, 1994) notes
that a critical factor in the long-term development of urban services is the existence of satisfactory
intergovernmental fiscal relations. This issue has received substantial attention in the preparation of
the project. A municipal finance study was prepared and released to the government for discussion as
a technical note. This study found that reform of intergovernmental fiscal relations in Latvia has
moved quite rapidly and that the municipal finance framework is more than adequate to support the
project and long term development of the sector. (See also "Latvia: Local Government Expenditures
and Resource Transfers.") Other municipal projects have also demonstrated the importance of cost
recovery to sustainability, the need for financial covenants to ensure enterprise viability and the need
for operational reforms to increase efficiency (including billing and collections, maintenance and
operations practices and demand management).



- 8 -
2.12          Loans made to Latvia to date include a Rehabilitation Loan, the Agriculture
Development Project, the Enterprise and Financial Sector Restructuring Project, the Liepaja
Environment Project and the Jelgava District Heating Project. These projects have demonstrated the
value of targeted technical assistance to facilitate procurement, construction supervision and
implementation of institutional strengthening programs as well as policy reforms. These projects have
also shown the need to size investments in accordance with affordability considerations.
E. Project Description
Riga Urban Transport
2.13          New Buses. Many of the buses held by Imanta and Talava are too old for economic
use. The purchase of 36 new articulated buses (18 each) and the disposal of 95 of the oldest buses (49
Talava, 46 Imanta) would reduce the age profile of their fleets, would permit maintenance staff
reductions and would effect substantial maintenance cost savings. Enhanced reliability of the fleets
would improve ticket revenue and would decrease passenger waiting times. Replacement of older
vehicles would result in decreased emissions and greater fuel economy.
2.14          New Trolleybuses. Many of the trolleybuses held by the Tram and Trolleybus Park
(TTP) are too old and too small for economic use. The purchase of 30 new large and more energy
efficient articulated trolleybuses will permit the disposal of some 56 of the oldest and small
trolleybuses with no loss of passenger carrying capacity. Also since the fleet is larger than required
to meet the needs of peak operation, it will be possible to remove an additional 37 uneconomic
trolleybuses from service. The overall benefits will be a reduction in drivers and maintenance staff
(to be accomplished through attrition), and substantial savings in maintenance costs and energy
consumption. Since the new trolleybuses will be able to use the existing overhead power transmission
system, the marginal costs are equivalent to those of new buses.
2.15          Tram Electrical Equipment. The majority of TTP tramns use outmoded traction control
systems which are unreliable and do not make the most effective use of energy. The purchase of 60
thyristor chopper traction control systems, suitable for the newer trams in the fleet, will produce
energy savings, reduce down-time, reduce maintenance of accelerator units, and increase reliability
leading to increased ticket revenue.
2.16          Capital Repair Programs. A review of the Imanta and Talava bus fleets undertaken
by consultants found many buses to be in poor condition but not sufficiently bad to justify expensive
replacement in light of current financial performance. Instead, it is proposed that 80 buses at each
enterprise should be rehabilitated making use of existing maintenance staff and repair facilities, and
the purchase of the necessary spares and materials as part of the project. The program is expected to
extend the life of these buses by 5 years and postpone the need for new buses. In the meantime
rehabilitation will reduce maintenance cost and improve reliability leading to extra revenue. TTP
already undertakes a limited program of capital repairs. The provision of spares and materials will
enable this program to make badly needed improvements to bodywork structures.
2.17          Spare Parts. All three enterprises suffer from a serious shortage of spares. As a
result many vehicles are out of service for long periods. Some spares are made in their own
workshops; however, most have to be imported. The enterprises, with the help of consultants, have
drawn up schedules of vital spares. The purchase of these spares by the project for general



- 9 -
maintenance and capital repairs will improve reliability and reduce the wasteful down-time of vehicles
leading to reduced costs and increase passenger revenues.
2.18          Relocation of Imanta Workshop. The Imanta maintenance facilities are located on
three separate sites. This leads to inefficient control of staff and is wasteful of resources as vehicles
have to be shunted from site to site at additional cost. The project provides for the closure of one of
the sites and the building of a new workshop at the main depot.
2.19          Workshop Plant and Equipment. Some of the plant and equipment used by the
enterprises is fully depreciated and needs replacement. Schedules and specifications have been drawn
up with the help of consultants. The new plant and equipment will increase operational efficiency,
reduce manpower requirements, and increase reliability leading to increased revenue.
2.20          Training and Technical Assistance. The project will provide the Riga City Council
and the three public transport enterprises with training and technical assistance to: (i) facilitate
implementation of the project including procurement; (ii) oversee a pilot program to evaluate
proposed implementation of a new ticketing system; (iii) oversee implementation of agreed action
plans; and (iv) support capacity building of the Public Transport Unit (PTU) and its implementation
of policy reforms agreed to and enacted by the Riga City Council in the attached Public Transport
Policy Statement (Annex A). The City of Riga has agreed to implement all measures outlined in
the Public Transport Policy Statement including payment of compensation due to the public
transport companies in connection with concessionary fares that may be required from time to
time by the City of Riga. [para 6. 1(a)]
Dau2avpils Water and Wastewater Treatment
2.21          Development of Ziemelu Wellfield. The Ziemelu wellfield has been investigated over
many years and numerous test boreholes have shown it to be a potential high quality water source.
The pipeline between Daugavpils and the site was started prior to Latvia's re-independence but
construction was stopped due to a lack of funds. The present project would allow for completion of
the pipeline, development of the wellfield to a capacity of 13,000 cubic meters per day, and
construction of facilities for iron removal. This would, in combination with rehabilitation of the
existing groundwater wellfield at Vingri with a capacity of 12,000 cubic meters per day, allow the
city to substantially reduce its dependence on surface water. The resulting diversification of raw
water sources would enable the utility to provide a minimal level of service during any future water
emergencies. In addition, it provides a base for future development of groundwater resources at
Ziemelu which will enable a complete transition away from surface water in the medium term.
2.22          In order to facilitate future groundwater resource development, fixed infrastructure at
the site is being developed in such a way as to allow a staged transition to full capacity. Parallel
development of the wellfield and iron removal facilities is necessary to ensure community support for
tariff increases necessary to restore the financial viability of the utility. In addition to increased water
safety and water quality, development of groundwater resources would result in lower operating costs
due to savings on water treatment chemicals.
2.23          Rehabilitation of the Existing Wastewater Treatment Plant.  The existing wastewater
treatment plant provides for primary treatment only. Rehabilitation of the existing mechanical



- 10 -
treatment works and addition of a biological phase would allow for significant reduction in levels of
biological oxygen demand (BOD) and suspended solids to compliance with EU standards.2
2.24          Rehabilitation of Pumping Stations. All of the flow to the sewage treatment works is
pumped, and over 90% of the sewage delivered at the treatment works is pumped by the Kandavas
pumping station. The two pumps at Kandavas were installed in 1963, and one of the pumps cannot
run for longer than two weeks at a time. Although there is relatively little problem with overflow at
present, there are concerns about the reliability of the pumps at Kandavas and at the satellite pumping
stations.
2.25          Metering. Although industrial customers are metered, most household and
institutional customers are not. Water consumption is calculated based on normatives which
significantly underestimate actual consumption (220 liters per person per day billed as compared to
280 liters per person per day consumed). This is true even though leakage in Daugavpils is a
relatively minor problem given the relative newness of most of the distribution network and limited
length of the distribution system relative to population. Under the project, funding has been included
for installation of meters in each of 200 apartment (block) buildings to allow for billing of actual
consumption. The project would also include financing for installation of bulk meters in the network
to monitor system performance and assist in leakage detection.
2.26          Monitoring. The project includes financing for laboratory equipment needed to
support monitoring of water and wastewater. The project also includes financing for installation of a
set of monitoring wells in the vicinity of the proposed Ziemelu wellfield to monitor water quality and
to monitor impact of pumping on direction of known contaminant plumes.
2.27          Institutional Strengthening and Twinning. The project includes funding for a twinning
arrangement which would include consultancy and advisory services aimed at assisting the enterprise
in shifting financial and operations management practices towards a more market-oriented and
commercial basis appropriate to an independent utility. The Finnish government has agreed to fund
this on a grant basis, and an agreement has been reached with the water utility of Tampere, Finland.
2.28          Technical Assistance for Project Management. Previous projects in Latvia have
shown the need for targeted technical assistance to assist the beneficiary in project management and
implementation including procurement, disbursement and construction supervision as well as
assistance in implementation of financial and operational changes required under the project.
Technical assistance is included to support the DMEU in these areas. Technical assistance is also
included to support development of detailed design for project implementation.
Municipal Development Fund
2.29          A Municipal Development Fund would be established on a pilot basis to finance
similar municipal infrastructure investments on a smaller scale in a number of communities
2Given space constraints on the existing site, the volume of retention tanks is limited. Discharge
standards have been estimated using these parameters and can meet HELCOM standards with the retention time
possible under these conditions. Movement to an entirely new site with no such space constraints is not
financially possible nor economically justified.



- 11 -
throughout Latvia. If successful, the MDF would provide a mechanism for channeling funds to local
governments at all levels for small and medium sized projects and would obviate the need for separate
preparation and appraisal by lending institutions of each individual project.
2.30          The MDF was established by decree of the Cabinet of Ministers on November 10,
1995. [para 6.2(a)] The MDF will be administered by the Public Investment Programll (PIP) unit in
the Ministry of Economy under the supervision of an inter-minlisterial Board of Directors. The Boardl
includes representatives of the Ministry of Finance, Ministry of Economy, Ministry of Environinenral
Protection and Regional Development and the Union of Local Gove arments. Representatives from
other sectoral Ministries will be invited to participate in the evaluation of projects in those sectors.
2.31          Project proposals would be developed and submitted by the municipality and
would be evaluated on the basis of criteria and procedures agreed with the Bank. (See attached
Annex B). [para 6. 1(b)]. Only investment projects will be financed. Areas eligible for financing
include water supply, wastewater collection and treatment, solid waste collection and disposal,
transport, district heating, construction of schools and medical facilities. Projects will be selected on
a demand driven basis and are not pre-identified. To be accepted, project proposals must be in the
sectors outlined above, must meet rate of return targets and financial covenants, show adequate
arrangements for project management and implementation and must present technical studies
demonstrating project feasibility and selection of least cost alternative.
Institutional Development Component
2.32          The Project will provide for technical assistance to the Borrower as required for
implementation of the MDF and for the Project Coordination Unit. This component also includes
financing for purchase of related equipment needed for the establishment of these units.
F. Cost Estimates
2.33          The total cost of the project is estimated to be US$45.4 million (Ls. 22.7 million
equivalent) including price and physical contingencies; US$33.9 million or 75% are foreign costs and
US$11.5 million or 25% are local. Price contingencies are based on local inflation rates of 15% in
1996, 10% in 1997 and 6% thereafter. International inflation is estimated at 2.6%. Physical
contingencies were estimated at 10% for project components involving construction and civil works.
No contingencies have been calculated on the MDF component as the sum allocated is a flat amount.
Total contingencies represent US$4.9 million or 11% of total project costs. (Contingencies represent
US$4.1 million or 18% of the costs of the Daugavpils component). Project cost estimates do not
include taxes. Amounts to be expended on VAT are not included in the project cost estimates, nor on
the following table. It is expected that VAT will be levied on local costs and will amount to an
additional US$1.7 million.

G. Project Financing Arrangements
2.34             The proposed Bank loan of US$27.3 million would finance approximately 60%  of
total project costs. The loan would finance US$23.0 million of foreign costs (68% of total foreign
costs net of taxes) and US$4.3 million of local costs (37% of total local costs net of taxes). The
Bank loan would be repaid over a seventeen year period including four years of grace at the Bank's
standard variable interest rate and standard commitment fees. Cofinancing in the amount of US$11.0
million has been confirmed for the Daugavpils component. A summary of the proposed financing
arrangements can be found in Table 2.2.
2.35             It was agreed that the Ministry of Finance would on-lend funds for the Daugavpils
water and wastewater component to the DMEU with a municipal guarantee of repayment. The
Ministry of Finance will on-lend funds for the Riga urban transport project to the City of Riga
directly for use by the three transport companies. The City of Riga will lend the proceeds to the
companies. Proceeds of the MDF would be on-lent at roughly the same terms and conditions as these
funds are provided to the Governmient of Latvia. It has been agreed that the interest rate mark-up
would be a minimum of 10% of the Bank interest rate and a maximum of 30% of the Bank interest
rate. Execution of subsidiary loan agreements with Riga and DMEU and the City of Daugavpils



- 13 -
would be required as conditions of effectiveness. [paras 6.3 (a) and (b)] The Ministry of Finance
would on-lend funds directly to beneficiaries of the MDF. Terms and conditions for sub-loans as
well as more details on individual financing packages for the various sub-projects are described
below.

2.36           Riga Urban Transport. The foreign component of the Riga Urban Transport sub-
project costs is relatively high at 97% given the emphasis on purchase of imported equipment and
extremely limited civil works. The municipality would be required to contribute 10% of total project
costs or US$1.9 million towards the project costs of which US$1.3 million would be foreign exchange
costs.
2.37           Daugavpils Water and Wastewater. The Bank loan would finance US$6.9 million or
approximately 31 % of total sub-project cost. NEFCO would provide a loan of US$2 million
equivalent (9% of total sub-project costs) for fifteen years at an interest rate of 0.5% over NEFCO's
cost of borrowing.
2.38           Donors involved in the Baltic Sea program have made commitments for grant
financing of US$9.0 million or 40% of the Daugavpils water and wastewater component of the
proposed project. The Swedish International Development Agency (US$3.0 million) and the Danish
Environmental Protection Agency (US$2.2 million) have committed joint financing of foreign
expenditures associated with the rehabilitation of the wastewater treatment plant and rehabilitation of
the pumping stations and gravity sewers subject to the approval of their respective governments. EU
PHARE has committed US$2.6 million which can be used for financing of the wastewater component
of the project. The Finnish government has committed to financing the twinning arrangement
(US$1.2 million). A condition for disbursement of the Daugavpils component would be that all
conditions be met for NEFCO co-financing and for grant financing by the Governments of
Sweden, Denmark, Finland and EU PHARE. [para 6.4(a)]
2.39           The Government of Latvia has committed US$2.0 million through the Public
Investment Program towards rehabilitation of the Daugavpils wastewater collection and treatment



- 14 -
systems. This financing would be on a grant basis and would be used to finance local costs. This
represents approximately 10% of total sub-project costs.
2.40         The municipality would contribute US$1.8 million and the water and wastewater
company US$0.5 million towards project costs met. Daugavpils' contribution to the financing
package has been set so that: (i) donor requirements regarding financing of the wastewater
component are satisfied; (ii) a minimum of 10% of total component costs are covered by the
municipality; (iii) total local counterpart financing (state and local) for the component is a minimum
of 20%; and (iv) total debt does not exceed estimated capacity for repayment.
2.41         Municipal Development Fund. Financing for the MDF is relatively modest as it is
intended to be a pilot, but should be sufficient for several moderate size projects. Total Bank
financing is US$3.5 million. Total local financing is estimated based on a maximum MDF
contribution of 20% towards sub-project costs. The selection of the first three projects and any
projects over US$500,000 will be subject to the agreement of the Bank.
2.42         Institutional Development Component. The project includes technical assistance for
consultant services and for equipment needed to assist the MDF and Project Coordination Unit
(PCU). US$100,000 of the loan proceeds has been allocated to the PCU and US$100,000 has been
allocated to the MDF. Repayment of these funds would be covered out of the interest rate mark-up
proposed by the Ministry of Finance.
H. Procurement
2.43         Procurement for Bank financed components would be governed by World Bank
Procurement Guidelines of January 1995 and would make use of the Bank's standard bidding
documents. Procurement arrangements are summarized in Table 2.3 and details of the procurement
plan are located in Annex C. The general procurement notice will be issued on November 30, 1995.
A procurement workshop was conducted during the appraisal and a project launch workshop will be
conducted in February 1996. A Country Procurement Review Paper has been prepared.
2.44         Responsibility for Procurement. Procurement will be coordinated by the PCU in the
Ministry of Environmental Protection and Regional Development (MOERD) which will be assisted by
procurement consultants, located in the Ministry of Finance, experienced in Bank procurement
procedures.
2.45         Procurement Procedures. For World Bank financed components the following
procedures are proposed.
(a)   International Competitive Bidding (ICB) will apply to all contracts for goods,
equipment and civil works greater than US$300,000. For the three major project
components it is expected that there will be 9 ICB contracts of which 7 are for goods,
1 for civil works and 1 supply and install. Total value of ICB contracts is expected to
be US$22.6 million for the two pre-identified sub-projects. Prequalification of
bidders will be required for the one supply and install contract and for the thyristor
chopper traction control systems.



- 15 -
(b)    National Competitive Bidding (NCB) will apply to civil works contracts greater than
US$50,000 and less than US$300,000. Bank sample bidding documents will be used.
Contracts involving NCB are expected to amount to US$3.4 million.
(c)    International Shopping (IS) will apply to contracts for equipment valued at less than
US$300,000 except as provided for below under local shopping. Contracts for IS will
be awarded on the basis of quotations to be obtained from a minimum of 3 suppliers
from at least 2 countries. This is expected to apply to contracts totalling US$2.0
million in the aggregate.
(d)    National Shopping (NS) will be used for items or groups of items costing less than
US$50,000 up to an aggregate of US$120,000. Award of contracts will be based on
at least 3 quotations obtained from three different eligible suppliers.
(e)    Consultant Services required under the project are expected to require US$1.8 million.
Consultant services would be procured in accordance with Bank Guidelines on the Use
of Consultants. Consultants would be shortlisted and selected on a competitive basis
with the exception of US$160,000 in short-term individual consultant services for the
MDF and PCU.
(f)    Direct Contracting will apply to purchase of some spare parts for the Riga urban
transport component of the project which have been identified as unique to trams,
trolleybuses and buses now in use and where substitution is not possible or where
compatibility of equipment cannot be demonstrated. Contracts involving direct
contracting are expected to amount to US$1.2 million.
2.46          In accordance with Bank procurement procedures, a preference for domestic
manufactured goods will be allowed equal to the applicable tariff for the nonexempt importer or 15%
of the CIF price of the imported goods offered by the competing bidders, whichever is lower. This
applies only to ICB contracts for goods.
2.47          Investments financed on a parallel basis by the Danish Government, the Swedish
Government, the Finnish Government, EU PHARE and NEFCO would be subject to the individual
procurement practices of those countries and organizations. In the case of the donors, most financing
is tied. Local counterpart financing from state and local governments would be subject to the
procurement requirements of the World Bank except where it is being applied towards a sub-
component of the project subject being financed by a donor or co-financier with distinct procurement
requirements which would govern that particular contract.
2.48          Prior Bank review will be required for all civil works and goods contracts over
US$300,000. Prior Bank review will be required for the first three NCB contracts for civil works.
Prior Bank review will be required for all consultant contracts greater than US$100,000 for firms and
above $50,000 in the case of individual consultants. However, regardless of value, all consultant
terms of reference will be subject to prior Bank review. Any contract awarded after direct
negotiations with suppliers would also be subject to Bank prior review. Prior review is expected to
cover US$44.8 million or 98% of the amount financed by the Bank and associated local counterpart
financing.

2.49          Procurement Monitoring and Reporting. Procurement data would be collected and
recorded by the PCU for: (a) prompt reporting of contract award information by the Borrower; (b)
comprehensive semi-annual reports to the Bank by the Borrower; and (c) revised timing of
procurement actions, including advertising, bidding, contract award, and completion time for
individual contracts. A computerized procurement management system would be installed by the
PCU.
I. Disbursement
2.50          It is expected that the loan will disburse starting in calendar year 1996 and finish in
year 2000. No standard disbursement profiles exist for municipal projects in Latvia or in the region.
The disbursement schedule for the Bank loan has been calculated separately for each component and
together in Table 2.4. Disbursement against specific categories is noted in Table 2.5. The Riga
urban transport component of the project is expected to disburse relatively quickly given the nature of
the project. Construction works for the Daugavpils component are not expected to commence until
the spring of 1997 and are estimated to require only two full construction seasons to complete given
the relatively modest nature of the works. The government has requested a Project Preparation
Facility (PPF) in the amount of US$510,000 to allow for initiation of detailed designs related to the
Daugavpils component and to allow for establishment of the PMUs.
2.51          Disbursement will be the responsibility of the Ministry of Finance. However, the
PMUs will be responsible for preparing and maintaining all documentation. Disbursement training
will be organized for staff in the PMUs and the Ministry of Finance.
2.52          To facilitate project implementation, the Borrower would establish a Special Account
in a major commercial bank, on terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank, to cover the Bank's
share of expenditures. The authorized allocation would be US$500,000, representing about four
months of average expenditures made through the Special Account. During the early stage of the
project, the initial allocation to the Special Account would be limited to US$250,000. However,
when the aggregate disbursements under the Loan have reached the level of US$2,000,000, the initial
allocation may be increased up to US$500,000 by submitting the relevant application for withdrawal.
Applications for replenishment of the Special Account would be submitted monthly or when one third
of the amount has been withdrawn, whichever occurs earlier. Documentation requirements for
replenishment would follow the standard Bank procedure as described in the Disbursement Handbook,
Chapter 6. Monthly bank statements of the Special Account, which have been reconciled by the
Borrower, would accompany all replenishment requests.

J. Accounts and Audit
2.54             For each of the sub-projects, separate project accounts would be required to be
maintained according to accepted international accounting standards. The purpose of separate project
accounts is to ensure that the flow of funds flows associated with the project can be identified and
tracked. These project accounts would be subject to annual audit along with the special accounts, as
described above. Audit would be undertaken by qualified independent auditors acceptable to the Bank



- 19 -
and in accordance with international accounting and auditing procedures. The MDF would also be
audited on an annual basis by qualified independent auditors acceptable to the Bank. Annual audits
for the two major sub-projects and for the MDF would be presented to the Bank no later than 6
months following the completion of the fiscal year. Audits of MDF sub-projects should be submitted
to the MDF no later than 4 months following the completion of the fiscal year.
K. Environmental Aspects
2.55         Preparation of the project has included environmental studies and reviews as required
under the Bank Operational Directive 4.01, "Environmental Assessment," and as required by Latvian
Law.
2.56         Daugavpils Water and Wastewater. This component involves improved water supply
for Latvia's second largest city and rehabilitation of the existing wastewater treatment plant in
Daugavpils. Both will have a beneficial environmental effect by: (i) providing safer supplies of
drinking water thus enhancing public health; and (ii) by reducing discharges of untreated and partially
treated wastewater to the Daugavpils River which will contribute to improved conditions in the Riga
Bay and Baltic Sea. An environmental review has been prepared in accordance with the requirements
for a "B" project which assesses the impact of discharge from the wastewater treatment plant and
which has been used to support the design of the component. The study also reviews previous
investigations of water quality at the proposed wellfield development and evaluates potential risk
factors. The findings of these reports are summarized and incorporated into the report in the
Environmental Review (Annex D). By January 31, 1996, DMEU will present a plan acceptable to
the Bank for addressing all issues highlighted in the Environmental Review: (i) establishment of
a groundwater monitoring program for the Ziemelu wellfield; (ii) improvement in sludge
management; (iii) establishment of a monitoring program for effluent from the wastewater
treatment plant and downstream river water quality; and (iv) remediation of the former Krizi
landfill to protect against any threat to the future quality of groundwater. A general condition
of the Daugavpils Project Agreement is that DMEU take all measures necessary to comply with
the agreed monitoring plan. [para 6. 1(c)]
2.57         Riga Urban Transport. This component has been prepared, as have other urban
transport projects in the Region, consistent with a "B" rating. An environmental study has been
completed with Dutch assistance (Annex E). This component is expected to have a positive impact on
the environment by reducing air pollution and energy consumption. Buses purchased under the
project will be required to meet higher emissions standards than the very old buses being withdrawn
from service, thus reducing the level of air pollution. The City of Riga already undertakes an
extensive program of emissions monitoring for buses and requires use of fuel meeting European
standards. Continuation of this program will be required as a general condition under the Riga
Project Agreement. [para 6. l(d)]. The sub-project, through introduction of improved maintenance
practices, will increase energy efficiency of the buses and reduce harmful emissions. Improvements
to trolleybuses and to tram traction systems will reduce energy consumption. Finally, the project is
designed to make public transport more attractive, thus reducing the rapid trend towards the use of
private cars and taxis. This would result in significant energy savings and reduced air pollution.
2.58         Municipal Development Fund Sub-projects would be required to meet both the
requirement of the Latvian Law on "State Environmental Impact Assessments" and Bank Operational
Directive 4.01.



- 20 -
L. Participatory Approach
2.59         The preparation of the project has been carried out in close cooperation with the
Ministry of Environmental Protection and Regional Development, the City of Riga, the Riga public
transport companies, the City of Daugavpils, the Daugavpils Municipal Enterprise Udensvads, the
Public Investment Program unit and the Ministry of Finance. Preparation of the Daugavpils Water
and Wastewater sub-project was carried out under the supervision of a steering group including
representatives of the Latvian authorities, the Swedish International Development Agency, the Danish
Environmental Protection Agency, the Finnish Ministry of Environment, EU PHARE, NEFCO and
the World Bank. Preparation of the Municipal Development Fund sub-project has been supervised by
a steering group including representatives of the Latvian Union of Local Governments.



- 21 -
III. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS
A. Organization and Management
3.1          Project Implementation Responsibilities. Primary responsibility for project
implementation would be decentralized to local authorities. This is consistent with one of the main
project objectives - namely to help strengthen management capacity at the local government level for
key municipal services. An individual Project Management Unit or PMU has been established for
each of the subprojects. These units are responsible for preparation of designs, specifications,
construction supervision, institutional strengthening, implementation of policy reforms required under
the project, project reporting and project accounts. A Project Coordination Unit (PCU) has been
established under the Ministry of Environmental Protection and Regional Development to oversee
implementation and to coordinate procurement and reporting. Technical assistance needed for project
implementation is included in the project cost estimates.
(a)    Riga Urban Transport. Project management responsibilities would be assigned to the
Public Transport Unit (PTU) which has been created through addition of staff with
requisite financial, economic, procurement and legal skills to the RBMST. The City
of Riga shall maintain the PTU with functions and responsibilities acceptable to
the Bank and with staff in adequate numbers, with qualifications, experience and
terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank. [para 6.1(e)]. The PTU would be
assisted by international consultants financed under the project.
(b)    Daugavpils Water and Wastewater. A PMU has been established in the Daugavpils
Municipal Enterprise Udensvads (DMEU). During execution of the project, DMEU
shall maintain the PMU with functions and responsibilities acceptable to the Bank
and with staff, in adequate numbers, with qualifications, experience and terms of
reference satisfactory to the Bank. [para 6.1(f)]. The PMU would be assisted by
international consultants financed under the project and would also benefit from a
twinning arrangement being set up with grant financing from the Finnish Government.
(c)    Municipal Development Fund. The MDF has been established by decree of the
Cabinet of Ministers. The Public Investment Program (PIP) unit of the Ministry of
Economy would administer the MDF under the supervision of a Board of Directors
consisting of representatives of the Ministries of Economy, Finance, Environment and
Regional Development, and the Union of Local Governments. Additional staff with
experience in civil engineering and project finance would be required to enable the
PIP unit to discharge these responsibilities and these costs would be covered through
an administrative mark-up on the on-lending rate. Actual implementation of
investment projects financed by the MDF would be the responsibility of the respective
local governments and their associated municipal enterprises or departments which
would appoint a project coordinator.
(d)    Project Coordination Unit (PCU). A Project Coordination Unit (PCU) has been
established under the Ministry of Environmental Protection and Regional Development
to monitor project implementation, coordinate procurement and to track progress of
disbursement and compliance with project conditionality. The unit consisting of at
least two experienced staff with civil engineering and financial backgrounds would



- 22 -
also be responsible for maintaining relations with the World Bank and donors and
assisting in project monitoring and supervision. During the execution of the
Project, the Borrower shall maintain the PCU with functions and responsibilities
acceptable to the Bank and with staff in adequate numbers with qualifications,
experience and terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank. [para 6. 1(g)].
3.2          Implementation Schedule. The proposed project would be implemented over a five
year period with an additional six months for the payment of financial obligations committed prior to
the project completion date and full withdrawal of Loan proceeds. The project is expected to be
completed by December 31, 2000, and the Loan closed on June 30, 2001. The PCU would submit
an Implementation Completion Report (ICR) to the Bank within six months of the closing date.
B. Project Reporting
3.3          A Project Implementation Plan has been developed including key project milestones
and timetables (Annex C). These would be monitored by the PCU with the assistance of staff in the
Daugavpils PMU, Riga PTU and in the MDF. The PCU would be responsible for submitting semi-
annual reports on project performance to the World Bank in collaboration with PMU, PTU and MDF
staff. The PCU would also be responsible for submitting similar semi-annual reports on project
performance to other donors and co-financiers. A mid-term review by the Bank would take place
no later than September 30, 1998. [para 6. 1(h)].
C. Project Supervision
3.4          In addition to review of procurement actions, audits and semi-annual reports on
project progress, the Bank will conduct regular supervision missions. It is also likely that donors and
co-financiers will participate in these supervision missions. A supervision plan has been elaborated in
the Project Implementation Plan. Given that two cities are involved in the project and both are
inexperienced with project management and procurement, at least 22 staff weeks per year will be
required for project supervision during the first two years of implementation.



- 23 -
IV. FINANCIAL AND OPERATIONAL ASPECTS
A. General Principles
4.1            Central issues in the development of municipal services in Latvia are movement
towards cost recovery and reinforcement of the legal autonomy and budgetary independence of
municipal enterprises through formalization of contractual relationships and strengthening of
institutional structures. Introduction of measures to improve the financial status has been required
under the project. The present status of these efforts and future actions necessary are described in
more detail below for the Riga Urban Transport and Daugavpils Water and Wastewater components.
B. Past Financial Performance
4.2            User charges for municipal services are determined by the municipality for both
municipal enterprises and for municipal departments. Although local governments in Latvia have
already made substantial progress towards cost recovery, user charges in most cases still fall below
full cost recovery levels including depreciation and maintenance. At current levels, user charges do
not allow service providers to function on a commercially viable basis and to maintain appropriate
debt service coverage ratios. Special characteristics involving the identified components are detailed
below.
4.3            Riga Urban Transport. The 1994 operating performance of the three Riga municipal
transport companies is summarized in Table 4.1 below.
Table 4.1

- 24 -
4.4           In Riga, public transport is subsidized though not to the same degree as in other
transition economies. The ratio of ticket revenue to total expenditures amounts to 56% (Imanta);
67% (Talava); and 72% (Tram and Trolleybus Park). The deficit for each of the three Riga
municipal transport companies is covered in part by transfers or subsidies from the municipal budget
amounting to a total of Ls. 3.0 million for 1994 for the three companies. Ticket revenue plus
transfers are sufficient to cover basic cash outflows; however, they do not cover depreciation and
amortization. This amounted to Ls. 1.0 million for 1994 or approximately 9% of total expenditures
for all three municipal public transport companies. This represents depreciation on historic book
value only, not actual replacement costs. As a result, the public transport companies have not been
able to purchase new replacement vehicles or to maintain sufficient spares inventories as required for
normal maintenance and periodic refurbishment of equipment.
4.5           The under funding of the transport companies has persisted since re-independence and
the state of the fleet has continued to deteriorate. The situation is particularly critical with respect to
the two bus companies where the average age of buses is 7.7 years (Talava) and 9.5 years (Imanta) as
compared to an expected useful life of 10 years for an Ikarus bus. The average age of trolleybuses is
approximately 10 years, slightly less than the useful life of the Skoda vehicles. (To enable efficient
operation, the average age of the fleet should be equal to half of the useful life of the vehicle - 5
years in this case). The situation is less severe with respect to trams as these have useful lives of 20
years, so lack of new investment over the last five years has had a much less serious impact. Without
sufficient cash flow to begin retirement and replacement of aged vehicles, public transport services in
Riga will be severely compromised. Vehicle operating costs will increase and reliability decrease as
vehicle breakdowns become increasingly frequent.
4.6           There are a number of factors contributing to the poor financial condition of the
enterprises. First, tariff levels established by the municipality are not sufficient to cover depreciation
even though they do achieve basic operating cost recovery. Second, operators are not directly
compensated for carrying passengers at concessionary or reduced fares. Such passengers account for
30% to 40% of all trips and it is estimated that the cost to the municipal public transport enterprises
amounts to Ls. 1.6 million annually, assuming a 0.5 price elasticity of demand. Third, fare evasion
is estimated at 10% and is estimated to cost operators Ls. 0.8 million. Fourth, operating costs are
high due to the age of the vehicle fleet, frequent breakdowns, lack of spare parts, and high employee
turnover due to perversely defined incentive programs.
4.7           Daugavpils Water and Wastewater. Table 4.2 below provides a summary of the
operating performance of DMEU for the last two years.
4.8           The financial condition of DMEU is poor. User charges are sufficient to cover the
basic cash outflows without any transfers from the municipal budget. However, they do not cover the
full costs of operation and some wastewater treatment processes have been discontinued due to lack of
funds for purchase of chemicals and repair of equipment. Even after economizing on operations,
uncovered costs represent 16% of total expenditures. The significant accumulation of arrears by
industrial customers has further strained the cash flow of the enterprise and the absence of any
allowance for bad debt expense distorts the validity of reported accounting income (losses). Estimated
annual losses of Ls. 100,000 and increases in accounts receivable are covered by accumulation of tax
arrears and depreciation.
