V. MONITORING, EVALUATION AND REPORTING
A. Monitoring Indicators
9. Project monitoring indicators have been established in order to enable the tracking of project inputs on key
development objectives throughout the project cycle. At the mid-term review the need to adjust or restructure the
project design, within the context of its overall development objectives, would be based on the data received
from these indicators. The overarching development objective of the proposed Project is to improve the financial
and operational management of municipal services in Latvia. The key indicators that will be used to monitor the
anticipated amelioration in the provision of municipal services will be: (i) improved cost recovery; (ii) improved
financial health (as measured by the cash flow and return on assets); and (iii) improved operational performance
(as indicated by, for example, reduced effluent pollution and reduced unaccounted for water volumes in
Daugavpils, and reduced fare evasion and public vehicle breakdowns in Riga). These specific monitoring
indicators will be indicative of the improved performance of the municipal services. They, together with
associated indicators, are presented in the following tables.
10.  Given the essential role that program monitoring and evaluation play in determining the impact of a given
intervention on development objectives, a number of indicators would be used to monitor and evaluate progress
during the implementation of the Project. The commercial, financial and operational indicators presented in the
following tables would be used to monitor the respective sub-projects.
11. Municipal Development Fund: The financial, commercial and operational monitoring indicators for the
sub-projects identified and implemented under the Municipal Development Fund, would be agreed as each
potential sub-project is developed. The indicators would be used to monitor the progress and effectiveness of the
sub-projects by measuring parameters similar to those identified for the two main Project components described
above. The indicators adopted for additional sub-projects would have to be acceptable to the World Bank.

B. Progress Reporting
12. Throughout the execution of the Project, semi-annual reports shall be sent to the World Bank
from the project coordination units (Udensvads PMU, Riga City Council PMU and the Public
Investment Program PMU, respectively).The reports shall be in the format indicated below for the
two major project components. Reports for those sub-projects identified and implemented under the
Municipal Development Fund shall be produced on an ad hoc basis but shall be of a similar format to
the models described in this section, containing all the relevant information discussed below.
13. Each semi-annual report shall consist of: (i) an executive summary; (ii) two pages of text
describing progress, status, and problems encountered with the project component; (iii) updated
implementation schedules, based on those presented in this annex and revised to highlight any
differences between actual and anticipated project execution; (iv) fully completed monitoring indicator
tables; and (v) financial statements. These elements of the reports are described in more detail below.
(i) Executive Summary: A brief summary of the principal information contained in the report. The
summary should emphasize, for the six month period in question, significant project
developments, major problems encountered, corrective measures taken to overcome any
implementation difficulties, and unusual events. An opinion should be given as to the overall
progress and effectiveness of the implementation of the project component.s.
(ii) Project Status: The document shall report on the progress of the component's implementation.
Reference shall be made to the updated implementation schedules and any differences shall be
explained in the text. Problems encountered in project execution shall be discussed and solutions
proposed. Any other unusual events shall be presented. Staffing difficulties, both within the
implementing agency and/or project management unit themselves as well as those related to
consultants and contractors involved in the Project, should be reported. This section should
include information on any changes in project management arrangements and summaries on the
performance of engineering and other consultants retained under the Project. A written synopsis
shall be presented of the work carried out in the preceding six months and that to be undertaken
in the coming six months. In particular, for each of the sub-projects, direct reference shall be
made to the respective components listed below:
(a) Riga Urban Transport (with reference to associated Action and Business Plans):
Riga City Council: addressing fares evasion; compensation for fares concessions; redefining the
network after survey (in conjunction with all three enterprises); fare increases; electronic ticket
machine pilot scheme (in conjunction with the enterprises).
Imanta and Talava: investment in new buses; reduction of operating fleet; rehabilitation or capital
repairs; investment in spare parts; building new maintenance facility; investment in plant and
equipment; technical training for staff; administration and information.
TTP: investment in new trolleybuses; reduction of spare trolleybus fleet; reduction of spare tram
fleet; investment in spare parts; technical training for staff; conversion of tram traction systems;
investment in plant and equipment; administration and information.



ANNEX C
Page 71 of 71
(b) Daugavpils Water Supply and Wastewater:
Investment in all water supply and wastewater components; status of new Udensvads-municipality
performance contract; status of joint stock company venture; efficiency improvements in billing
and collection; reduction in unaccounted-for water; metering program; staff reduction / enterprise
efficiency improvement; time of response to water leaks, sewerage overflows, etc; potable water
quality; wastewater effluent quality and downstream river quality; implementation of tariff
increases; information technology / management improvements; status of twinning arrangement;
(iii) Implementation Schedules: The implementation schedules presented in this annex for the major
components of the Project will be made available to the respective implementing agencies as
software. In this way the execution of all the major components (together with all other smaller
components, for which the implementing agencies should create similar computer-based
schedules) will be effectively tracked and compared with the initial program. Graphic print outs
of the schedules should be appended to the semi-annual progress reports.
(iv) Monitoring Indicator Tables: The tables given above, which document changes in the
monitoring indicators, shall be reproduced for each report and incorporated as appendices.
(v) Financial Statements: Model forms for statements of income and expenditure are given above in
Section IV for each of the three Riga transport companies and for Udensvads, respectively.
Financial statements, based on these models and reporting on the previous six months
performance, shall be appended to the semi-annual reports.



ANNEX D
Page I of 7
DAUGAVPILS WATER AND WASTEWATER COMPONENT
ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
A.Overview
1.    Implementation of the proposed project is anticipated to have positive environmental benefits
through improvement of water quality in the River Daugava and reduced pollution of the Baltic Sea
due to significant improvements in the performance of the municipal wastewater treatment plant.
2.    The proposed project component was the subject of an environmental review prepared by Saur
International (France)/Dynamco (United Kingdom), working in association with Aqua Brambis
(Latvia). The review was consistent with the requirement of the World Bank for a Category B project
and complies with the Latvian Law on Environmental Impact Assessment. The objective was to
evaluate the potential environmental impact from the reconstruction and rehabilitation program to be
carried out by Daugavpils Municipal Enterprise Udensvads (DMEU) under the project. The study
was conducted using a site-specific approach.
3.    The environmental review of the proposed project was prepared with the full cooperation of
the Latvian Ministry of Environmental Protection and Regional Development (MOERD) and its
Daugavpils Regional Environmental Protection Committee. Representatives of the MOERD and its
Environmental Assessment Board have been involved in all stages of development of the proposed
project and have participated in a series of visits to all proposed program sites. Consultations have
been conducted with the representatives of the local municipality and DMEU.
B. Present Conditions
4.    Water supply. Both surface water (70%) and groundwater (30%) resources are exploited for
drinking water supply. Treated water complies with WHO and EU standards most of the time, and
quality problems (levels of iron, manganese, aluminum and THMs) are not considered to be a public
health issue although consumers are dissatisfied with the unpleasant color and odor of the water.
COD levels exceed EU guidelines for water to be treated to the highest standard. The oldest of the
two surface water treatment plants dates from 1889 and is in poor physical condition; the design does
not permit improvement. Chlorinators at both surface water treatment works are in poor condition
and represent a potential risk. Unsafe storage of large volumes of chlorine also poses a threat to
surrounding workers and inhabitants and should be addressed.
5.    At present, pollution incidents upstream of Daugavpils could have significant implications for
water supply especially as there is no upstream monitoring/warning system on the River Daugava and
there is no significant storage capacity within the water supply network should a production shutdown
be necessary as a result of a serious upstream pollution incident. The situation is exacerbated because
the Daugava is an international river and potential pollution sources exist upstream of Daugavpils in
Belarus and Russia as well as at Kraslava in Latvia and the Ignalina Nuclear Power Plan in Lithuania.
Chemical factories situated near the border represent a particular hazard. In November 1990, the
surface water treatment plants (Gajok 1 and 2) had to be closed for ten days due to a cyanide spill
(130 tons) in Novopolotsk, Belarus. Low level pollution occurs continuously.
6.    Leakage in the distribution network is reportedly very low (8%). This has been verified by
independent field tests and is lower than DMEU internal estimates. Factors explaining the unusually
low leakage are: limited extent of the "external" distribution pipework at 2.23 m/person (i.e. all that



ANNEX D
Page 2 of 7
pipework outside of the apartment blocks) due to the concentration of the population in large block
housing estates; low night pressures; and low relative age of the distribution network.
7.     Wastewvater Collection and Treatment. The cumulative length of the sewerage system in
Daugavpils is about 180 km and about 90% of the population is connected to the system. Leakage of
sewage to the surrounding soil through worn-out sewers is a potential threat to the quality of
groundwater. The infiltration of groundwater into sewers is significant and has also resulted in
overflows. Works aimed at the rehabilitation of these sewers should be given high priority to reduce
excessive hydraulic loads on the treatment plant and to minimize groundwater pollution.
8.     The Kandavas pumping station is responsible for the delivery of approximately 90% of the
treatment works' flow. The two pumps were installed in 1963 and one of the pumps cannot run for
more than two weeks at a time. Both are in need of urgent replacement. Many of the nineteen
satellite pumping stations are also in poor condition and require rehabilitation or replacement.
9.     Most of the municipal wastewater of Daugavpils is treated at plants consisting of only
preliminary and primary treatment with no biological phase in which the reduction of oxygen.-
consuming materials and of nutrients is poOI The current low level of wastewater treatment is one of
the major sources of pollution of the River Daugava and also has significant impact on the Gulf of
Riga. Daugavpils has been identified as one of the three priority pollution "hotspots" in Latvia under
the Baltic Sea Program.
10.   Sludge from the settling tanks is collected in two thickeners and taken by tanker for disposal
on sludge fields at the Krizi landfill site. The sludge treatment process needs to be improved to
reduce the volume of sludge, increase sludge stability, and develop a strategy for its final disposal and
utilization.
C. Elements of the Proposed Project
11.   A brief description of the proposed water supply and wastewater treatment improvements to
be carried out under the proposed project in Daugavpils is given below:
(a)    Water Supply
(i)    Bulk and block metering. Installation of bulk meters to improve the data on
network performance and to control water use. Installation of block meters
will enable more accurate billing for household consumption which is
currently under-invoiced due to underestimation of billing nonnatives.
(ii)    Vingri Equipment. To provide critical spare parts to maintain the current
plant performance for one of two existing groundwater abstraction plants.
(iii)   Ziemelu Well Field and Treatment Plant. To develop groundwater well field
to a minimum capacity of 13,000 m' per day with potential for eventual
expansion up to a maximum of 33,000 m3 per day; to complete pipeline from
site to main distribution network; and to construct iron removal plant.



ANNEX D
Page 3 of 7
(b)    Wastewater Treatment
(i)    Rehabilitation of Kandavas Pumping Station. Replacement of pumps to
increase reliability and minimize the possibility of flooding.
(ii)   Rehabilitation of Secondary Pumping Stations. Replacement of the most
dilapidated pumps in the nineteen satellite pumping stations to increase
reliability and minimize the possibility of flooding.
(iii)   Rehabilitation of Sewers. Reconstruction of worn-out sewers to limit
groundwater inflow and to minimize the potential for contamination of
groundwater by raw wastewater.
(iv)   Rehabilitation and Extension of Treatment Plant. Upgrading of the treatment
facilities to include a biological phase for the removal of organics and
nutrients (phosphorus and nitrogen) to meet HELCOM recommendations.
D. Environmental Impact
12.   The environmental impact which may result from the proposed water and wastewater
treatment has been divided into general impacts and specific impacts.
13.   General Impacts. There will inevitably be some environmental impacts associated with
construction activities. The magnitude of these impacts will depend on a number of factors including
proximity of houses, length of construction period, working hours and methods of construction. Such
impacts from large construction works (e.g., extension to the wastewater treatment works, Ziemelu
source development and iron removal plant) have the potential to be significant over a two year
period. In particular such impacts could include: noise and vibration; dust release; possible odor
release when working on existing sewerage and sewage treatment facilities; pollution of surface or
underground water due to release/spillage of material from construction sites; disruption of traffic and
noise pollution associated with transporting construction materials and large items of plant; pollution
risk and hazard to the health of construction workers if construction is carried out on contaminated
land (e.g. areas where DMEU sewage sludge has been stored). Visual environmental impacts are
expected to be mninimal. None of the water or wastewater treatment proposals involve resettlement of
indigenous people.
14.   Specific Impacts of Water Supply Component:
(i)    Bulk and Block Metering, Demand management is expected to result in a
decrease of water use which would have a positive environmental impact as
less water would be needed to be abstracted thus saving energy and materials,
and less wastewater would be produced, thus minimizing the possibility of
pollution in general and reducing the energy and materials spent on
wastewater treatment;
(ii)    Vingri Equipment, Modern equipment is expected to be less energy
consuming, thus having a positive environmental impact; and
(iii)   Ziemelu Well Field and Treatment Plant. Improved water quality would have



ANNEX D
Page 4 of 7
a positive health impact, particularly after full exploitation of Ziemelu which
will enable complete transition away from surface water to groundwater. The
reduction in the dependency upon the River Daugava affords the consumers of
drinking water a greater level of protection from the negative health effects
associated with a sudden deterioration in the river water quality caused by
upstream pollution. The proposed phased implementation of the wellfield
development will be accompanied by monitoring of adjacent groundwater
levels. Any impacts on local river flows or wetland ecology will thus be
observed sufficiently early so that mitigation measures can be introduced to
counter any negative effects. Construction of the pipeline, groundwater wells
and access roads will require only limited tree clearing as the site has already
been partially developed. Land take associated with establishment of iron
removal facilities at Jauna Forstate and the new pipeline is not considered to
be significant, however ecological site inventories will be undertaken before
final site selection is agreed. Noise is not likely to be a significant issue given
experience with other similar facilities. Iron removal facilities will generate
some sludge but this is estimated to be minor in comparison with that from
the waste water treatment plant. As for the wastewater sludge, a suitable
management strategy will be developed for the water treatment works' sludge.
This strategy will be incorporated into the scope of the technical assistance for
the detailed design of the iron removal plant.
(iv)   The groundwater source to be utilized is, however, potentially vulnerable to
pollution from two contaminated areas: a former dump for municipal solid
waste in Krizi, located about 2 lkn south-east of Ziemelu, and an old military
oil depot about 3.5 km north-east of Ziemelu. These have been studied and
have been found to pose no threat to area groundwater supplies for at least a
thirty year period. However, further monitoring and groundwater modelling
will be required to ensure that any potential risks are detected early enough to
allow for prompt mitigation in order to protect the wellfield over the longer
term. Funding for these investigations are included in the financing estimates
for the project. The City of Daugavpils has agreed to repair the cover over
the closed Krizi landfill which will further protect against any future threat of
contamination. Appropriate wellhead protection zoning will be incorporated
into the wellfield design and will ensure that the groundwater quality is not
compromised by surface contamination.
15.   Specific Impacts of Wastewater Treatment Component:
(i)    Rehabilitation of Pumping Stations. Upgrading of pumping stations will
improve efficiency thus saving energy and reduce costs; more reliable pumps
will minimize the risk of malfunction and possible consequent environmental
contamination from overflowing sewage pumping stations.
(ii)    Rehabilitation of Sewers. Rehabilitation of sewers will reduce groundwater
pollution and infiltration thus reducing the amount of water to be pumped as
well as reducing the possibility of flooding.



ANNEX D
Page 5 of 7
(iii)   Rehabilitation and Extension of Wastewater Treatment Plant.
Extension/upgrading of the Daugavpils wastewater treatment plant will
improve effluent quality and thus enhance the physicochemical and
microbiological quality of the River Daugava, the Riga Bay and river bathing
beaches in the discharge area. During the feasibility study and environmental
review, the option of constructing an entirely new facility at a separate
location was reviewed but was found not to be financially feasible or
justifiable. Industrial pre-treatment has been evaluated through sampling
during the feasibility study and found to be sufficient to enable biological
treatment of wastewater. The introduction of a wastewater influent
monitoring system will be included to ensure that any abnormal influent
industrial discharges are identified early and diverted so that they do not
poison the biological treatment phase. A complete sludge strategy will be
included in the scope of the technical assistance for the detailed design of the
wastewater treatment plant. The upgraded wastewater plant will include
mechanical dewatering equipment for the reduction of the sludge volume.
Such reduction in volume will also reduce environmental impacts arising from
transportation of sludge. After dewatering, the sludge will be treated by
either lime stabilization or composting. The strategy will identify the most
suitable and environmentally acceptable final disposal/reuse route for the
treated sludge.
16.   Proposed Mitigation Plan. The overall aim of the project is to undertake measures which will
improve the water supply and wastewater services of Daugavpils. The major environmental impacts
of the project will therefore be beneficial in improving the health and quality of life of the city's
population. Further positive impacts will result from the improvement in water quality in the River
Daugava and Riga Bay. The impact of construction will primarily affect the human population and
will be largely limited to relatively minor disruption and disturbance. It would be possible to reduce
the extent of this inconvenience by implementing regular construction site practices and procedures.
17.   Although the expected impacts of the project are positive, actions by DMEU will be required
to: (i) monitor raw and treated water quality; (ii) monitor industrial wastewater discharges; (iii)
improve sludge management; and (iv) monitor receiving water quality. These measures are discussed
below.
18.   Water Quality Monitoring. The monitoring of water quality at the source and after treatment
is carried out by the utility. The required analyses are set by law and consist of more than twenty
parameters. The Ministry of Welfare, through its Environment Health Centers, are supervising the
performance of the monitoring and carrying out control measurements (after treatment) about once
every month and additionally at household taps on a random basis. The list of monitored parameters
do not cover certain toxic parameters which could have an impact on human health. A recent United
States Environmental Protection Agency (US EPA) study (financed by United States Agency for
International Development) has recommended the expansion of the list. The Ministry of Health and
MOERD are currently considering the review of the monitoring requirements. As soon as the new
recommendations are adopted they should be incorporated into the water quality monitoring program
at Daugavpils.
19.   The new groundwater field at Ziemelu provides an important opportunity to improve drinking
water quality in Daugavpils. However as mentioned above, the source is potentially vulnerable to



ANNEX D
Page 6 of 7
pollution from two sources, the former municipal solid waste dump at Krizi and the former military
oil depot. Utilization of the Ziemelu wellfield will alter groundwater flows and is expected to divert
the contaminated plumes towards Ziemelu. Additional wells will be drilled to allow for the monitoring
of the aforementioned pollution sources as the phased development of the wellfield proceeds.
20.    A wellhead protection program has been put into place with the assistance of the US EPA.
However there is, at present, no legislation to protect the recharge area of Ziemelu groundwater
wellfield from potentially polluting activities or to restrict the patterns of land use in the area. The
situation is expected to improve since the Law on Protected Areas is currently being discussed in the
Parliament and is expected to be passed soon. Nevertheless, certain activities in the recharge area
(such as the installation of subterranean oil tanks or the quarrying of sand) currently require permits
from the MOERD and would not be granted on the protected zones already established around
Ziemelu welifield.
21.    Wastewater Monitoring. According to current legislation, the water utility is responsible for
monitoring the wastewater quality in the collection network, in the treatment plant and after treatment.
The list of parameters that are monitored consists of about 20 compounds. The utility is also
responsible for monitoring the wastewaters discharged by those industries which are connected to the
public sewerage system. The Daugavpils Regional Environmental Protection Committee oversees
DMEU's monitoring program by taking their own samples and analyzing them at their own
laboratory. Although the list of parameters for the monitoring of industrial wastes is extensive, the
US EPA study has proposed to include some additional toxins, which potentially can accumulate in
fish and in the sediments of receiving waters.
22.    Due to the decline in industrial activity in Daugavpils, the problem of heavy metals in the
wastewater is no longer considered to be a problem. The monitoring/regulating system, however, was
not able to prevent the discharge of excessive industrial loads as the pollution fees were far too low to
be a deterrent to the industries. The monitoring/regulating system has mainly been used to collect the
associated pollution fees from contravening industries rather than improve the quality of the industrial
effluent that is discharged to the municipal sewers. Under this project, with the introduction of
biological treatment at the wastewater works, illicit discharges must be more closely monitored and
controlled to provide a barrier against the failure of the plant's biological phase. A review of the
current industrial discharge permitting system, and recommendation for its improvement, will be
included in the technical assistance for the design of the wastewater treatment plant. A wastewater
influent monitoring system will also be included in the design of the treatment works which will
ensure that any influent toxic slugs are diverted so that the biological treatment phase is not poisoned
by sudden, excessive, toxic polluting incidents.
23.    Sludge Management. The quality of the sludge produced at the wastewater treatment works is
not regularly monitored. Some studies indicate that the levels of heavy metals have decreased, along
with the aforementioned decline in industrial activity in Daugavpils, to levels which would allow the
safe reuse of the stabilized sludge on agricultural land. According to the utility, local farmers are not
interested in using the wastewater sludge on their fields due to the fear of, inter alia, industrial
contaminants and odor release. Currently sludge is dried on dedicated fields which are located
adjacent to Krizi dump. The desiccated sludge, following a 1-2 year drying period, is used as cover
material for both the old and the new landfills.
24.    The upgrading of the existing wastewater treatment plant will include mechanical dewatering
equipment for the sludge that will allow its dry solids content to be increased to about 20-25 percent.



ANNEX D
Page 7 of 7
Two options for stabilizing the dewatered sludge were investigated during the feasibility study:
(i) lime stabilization; and (ii) composting with peat and sand. Preliminary calculations showed the
lime stabilization to be the cheaper option. A final decision on the method of sludge stabilization will
be made during the detailed design phase. There is also a clear need to launch a public information/
education campaign with the local farmers in order to inform them of the benefits of the stabilized
sludge as a safe fertilizer and soil conditioner. Currently Latvia does not have any regulations
concerning the reuse of sludge in agriculture. It is, however, expected that before the wastewater
treatment plant is commissioned, preliminary national guidelines will be available. These issues,
related to the final disposal of sludge, will be incorporated into the scope of the technical assistance
for the detailed design of the wastewater treatment plant.
25.   Although the configuration of the upgraded wastewater treatment plant will only be defined
following the detailed design stage, the following approximate estimates have been produced
(assuming the incorporation of primary sedimentation tanks) as to the anticipated volume and solids
content that the plant will generate: (i) primary sludge, 5 tons per day at 3% dry solids; and
(ii) secondary sludge, 5 tons per day at 1 % dry solids. Assuming both sludges will be co-thickened in
the sludge thickeners, then 250 m3 of thickened sludge, at 5 % dry solids, will be produced daily,
which will be dewatered to produce 50 m3 of sludge at 20% dry solids each day.
26.   Monitoring of Receiving Water Ouality. The monitoring of water quality in receiving waters
is the responsibility of the Hydrometereological Agency. Currently the Agency is taking monthly
samples upstream and downstream of Daugavpils. The list of parameters which are monitored
monthly is extensive. Regional committees also carry out some monitoring of surface waters, but this
is less comprehensive. The aforementioned US EPA study recommended that some additional
parameters (organic chemicals) be included in the surface water monitoring list.
27.   It is proposed that data on surface water quality be collected prior to the commissioning of the
upgraded treatment plant. This information would be used as a baseline against which the anticipated
improvement of the River Daugava's water quality would be measured. Such a monitoring program
would involve the analysis of water samples from various points along the river. Ideally, baseline data
collection would commence a year before the plant is commissioned.
E. Agreed Actions
28.   It was agreed that DMEU would, with the assistance of the MOERD and the Daugavpils
Regional Environmental Protection Committee, submit a plan to address these issues no later than
January, 31, 1996. Such plan would be discussed and agreed with the Bank. DMEU will be
required to implement this plan and to take other such actions with regard to environmental
monitoring and mitigation as may reasonably be required by the Bank.

RIGA URBAN TRANSPORT COMPONENT
ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
A. Overview
1.    The implementation of the proposed project would have a positive environmental impact
through improvements that will increase use of public transport and limit the pace of growth use in
private auto transport. The project will also have a direct impact through reduced emissions and
reduced fuel consumption due to upgrading of the public transport fleet and to improved maintenance.
2.    An environmental review was prepared by the Dutch consultancy DHV in association with
Holland Transport Advising. The review is consistent with the requirement of the World Bank for a
category B project and complies with the Latvian Law on Environmental Impact Assessment.
B. Present Situation
3.    In the past few years the noise levels in Riga have increased due to an increase in transport
flows, bad road conditions and a decrease in technical conditions of most vehicles. Observations made
by the Environmental Health Dept., the Ministry of Welfare, in 1994, showed that, on main roads,
the actual noise levels are exceeding the standards (70 dB(A) during day time, 60 dB(A) during night
time) by between 10 to 20 dB(A). These standards were set in Soviet times.
4.    Vehicles are currently subject to annual roadworthy tests which would ensure that particularly
noisy buses would be kept off the road. A Law on Environmental Protection is currently being
discussed in the Latvian Parliament, although it is believed that this will have limited powers with
respect to abnormal noise levels. Enforcement of proposed regulations regarding noise are to become
the responsibility of the Municipality.
5.    The total emissions of NO,, CO and hydrocarbons for transport and stationary sources (boiler
houses - which produce hot water for apartments and factories, industrial sources etc.), for Latvia as
a whole, in 1993, are given below in Table 6.1. Air quality measurements have been undertaken in
the City of Riga. It has been agreed that the city would prepare an annual report on air quality.
Some typical concentrations measured in streets with heavy traffic are given below in Table 6.2. It
can be observed that the air quality standard with respect to NO,, is exceeded in streets with heavy
traffic in Riga. Generally, new cars are less polluting than old cars.

6.     Random vehicle testing, undertaken by Riga Technical University, on buses with both diesel
and petrol engines, indicated that smoke emission was high, due to bad regulation of engines. The
University stated that, on average, 40% of existing buses tested failed the emissions check. The
RBMST now subjects buses operated by the three Riga public transport companies to regular
emissions testing every quarter. Failed vehicles are kept out of service until repairs can be made.
These requirements will be expanded to include all privately licensed buses. Specifications for fuel
used by the three Riga public transport companies comply with European standards.
C. Environmental Impacts of the Proposed Project
7.     Tramns. As a consequence of the proposed investment in converting 60 trams to thyristor
chopper control, the energy consumption will be reduced (calculated reduction of approximately 4375
MWh per annum). This reduction in energy consumption will lead to a slight reduction in air
pollution from electric power plants. This reduction, however, will not be of any influence on air
quality in the city of Riga. Good and preventive maintenance leads to a reduction of noise nuisance.
Technical assistance to be provided, to improve maintenance procedures, will have an impact in this
area.
8.     Trolleybuses. It is proposed to renew part of the fleet (30 trolleybuses). As a consequence, it
is calculated that energy consumption will reduce by approximately 1984 MWh per annum. This
reduction in energy consumption will lead to a slight reduction in air pollution from electric power
plants. This reduction, however, will not be of any influence on air quality in the city of Riga.
9.     Buses. It is proposed to renew part of the fleet (36 buses). New buses are less noisy and less
polluting than old buses. Some emission factors for (Dutch) buses built in the year of 1970, 1980 and
1990 are given below in Table 6.3. Emission factors of, for example, Ikarus buses will probably not
be totally comparable, although it may be considered that, for engines of these type of buses, more
modern techniques will reduce the volumes of pollution, with respect to CO, hydrocarbons and
particles. The newer buses to be purchased will be supplied with engines of the Euro II specification,
which are much less polluting than existing buses. It should also be noted that the Danish consulting
firm, RH&H, will undertake a demonstration project in Latvia to indicate where reductions in
emissions can be made.

Good maintenance and engine regulation will minimise air pollution and noise. Technical assistance to
be provided, to improve maintenance procedures, will have an impact in this area.
10.    Wider impact. The proposed investments will assist in reversing the trend towards greater use
of private cars and is an important environment consideration. Table 6.3 details emission factors, with
respect to cars and buses. The information is based on data from the Netherlands.

11.    The use of 25 to 50 cars, instead of one bus, will result in much higher total emissions of CO
and hydrocarbons. The total emissions of NO, and particles are comparable. Noise levels generated
by 25/50 cars will also be higher than that produced by one bus.
12.    A further consequence of greater car use, instead of public transport, will be increases in
traffic volumes, with average vehicles speeds decreasing. This will result in more stops and increasing
accelerations, with subsequently higher emissions of all components and higher noise levels.

LATVIA
MUNICIPAL SERVICES DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. Riga Urban Transport
1.    Project Benefits. The proposed project would have significant economic benefits related to:
(a) reduced vehicle operating costs; and (b) passenger time savings. These benefits have been
calculated by comparing the "with" and "without" project scenarios. The assumptions used in this
analysis are further defined below.
(a)    Reduced vehicle operating costs. The state of equipment in the Riga public transport
companies has deteriorated significantly over the last five years given the lack of
investment in new equipment, maintenance and spare parts. The reduction in spares
budget has forced the companies into cannibalization of vehicles. Over time this
raises the cost of operating vehicles given the increasing number of breakdowns,
increased expenditure on maintenance, lower capacity utilization and increased fuel
consumption. Extensive corrosion of vehicles reduces structural integrity and
requires capital repairs to steel body works. Purchase of new vehicles, spare parts
inventories and maintenance equipment in combination with actions to retire the least
serviceable vehicles will enable a substantial reduction in maintenance costs (staff and
spare parts) and will reduce expenditures for fuel and energy.
The extent of these savings has been modelled for each individual measure based on
the assumption that costs would continue to increase at an average rate of 1 % per year
without the project. With the project, it is assumed that costs would fall initially by
10%. Costs for both scenarios are estimated in 1995 constant lats and have been
adjusted to remove taxes and to compensate for differences in prices. The most
significant adjustments are to: (i) increase the cost of electricity prices which have
been revalued to the external purchase price (roughly twice the current price paid by
the transport companies); and (ii) to remove the impact of the turnover tax (18%).
The government operates a floating exchange rate, with respect to the lat, and,
therefore, prices do not have to be adjusted in this respect.
Vehicle life is estimated at 12 years for buses and 15 years for trolleybuses.
Investments in tram thyristor chopper traction control systems are estimated to yield
benefits for 12 years given expected useful remaining life of the upgraded vehicles.
(b)    Passenger waitine time. Time savings are realized through a decrease in breakdowns
and vehicle availability, with an assumed passenger time loss of 5 minutes per
incident. The proposed investments in spare parts, machinery and training plus the
increased reliability of the new vehicles will improve the quality of the service
offered. In the "without project case," an annual increase in the number of
breakdowns and trips not operated was assumed to increase by 2% per annum. In the
"with project case," there is an initial decrease in breakdowns and trips not operated
by 90% and a further annual decrease of 1 % per annum due to better training and
additional new vehicle purchases. Non-work time was valued at 40% of work time.



ANNEX F
Page 2 of 5
The monthly wage was assumed to be 150 lats (significantly above the national
average) to account for the higher Riga incomes.
(c)    Terminal value. No terminal value has been assumed.
(d)    Additional benefits. Additional benefits resulting from the proposed investment were
not readily quantifiable and were not included in the analysis. These include:
reductions in vehicle congesion, increase in passenger mobility and improvement in
city image. This analysis also does not include any benefits due to the rationalization
of the network following completion of the passenger study.
2.    Project Costs. Costs used for estimating the economic rate of return are the total project costs
of the Bank project between 1996 and 2000 and additional investments made by the public transport
enterprises out of own source revenues to the year 2007. Estimates of local and foreign costs are in
1995 prices. Costs exclude price contingencies as these involve only inflation. No physical
contingencies are assumed on equipment purchases. Physical contingencies of 10% have been
assumed on civil works. As the portion of civil works in the sub-project are very small, this has little
impact. Local costs have been converted to US dollar equivalent using prevailing exchange rates.
3.    Economic Rate of Return. The economic rate of return was calculated by comparing the costs
and benefits of the two scenarios: "with project" and "without project." The economic rate of return
is 22%. Benefits due to passenger time savings are a small proportion of total project benefits (5%).
The net present value is US$8.8 million. A discount rate of 10% was used in calculating the net
present value. These calculations are shown in Table F-1.
4.    A sensitivity analysis was conducted to measure the impact of variations in costs and benefits
on the economic rate of return. The sensitivity was measured by calculating the switching values (the
extent to which costs or benefits would have to change to reduce the economic rate to 12%). Project
costs could be increased by a factor of 1.38 or project benefits reduced by a factor of .72 and the
economic rate of return would still remain at 12%. The impact of a potential devaluation of the
currency would be to an increase in project costs and a partial increase in project benefits. The
project would be able to maintain a 12% economic rate of return with a devaluation of 40%.
B. Daugavpils Water and Waste Water
5.    Project Benefits. The proposed project would have the following benefits: (a) public health
impacts from improved drinking water safety and quality; and (b) reduced discharge of untreated or
partially treated waste water to the River Daugava. An economic rate of return could not be
calculated as the benefits from both components are not easily quantifiable. Instead, a least cost
analysis of alternatives was conducted.
(a)    Improved drinking water safety and quality. Construction of a new groundwater
wellfield will enable diversification of water supplies and provide a basis for complete
movement away from surface water. Daugavpils is downstream from several major
chemical factories in Belarus and there have been several accidents involving
discharge of toxic chemicals into the river. In November 1990, 130 tons of cyanide
were discharged into the water and all surface water intakes in Daugavpils had to be
turned off for a period of 10 days. Daugavpils is also within close proximity to oil
storage facilities at Kraslava and the Ignalina nuclear power plant in Lithuania.



ANNEX F
Page 3 of 5
Diversification of water supply is expected to have significant positive public health
benefits and would reduce costs associated with provision of alternative water supply
during water emergencies.
Increased use of groundwater will also improve water quality. Iron, manganese,
aluminum, oxydizability and THM are higher than EU standards. While the iron and
other metals are mainly and aesthetic concern, the THMs are carcinogenic
compounds. Although they are common in most of the systems using raw water with
a high organic content, and the relation of cancer and THMs in drinking water is
difficult to prove, the general tendency is to move away from using contaminated or
otherwise high organic content surface waters to groundwater use or to use activated
carbon filters in combination with ozonation instead of chlorination.
The value of these benefits do not lend themselves to quantification, although it can be
reasonably assumed that the benefits are significant given the potential positive impact
on public health and the physical environment. Instead, a least cost analysis of
alternatives was conducted comparing transition to groundwater with use of activated
carbon filters and ozonation. The fixed investment costs of both alternatives was
comparable; however, the operating costs of upgrading the surface water treatment
works would be 25 % higher given the higher chemical costs. In addition, upgrading
the surface water treatment works would result in the city's continued vulnerability to
oil spills and accidental release of toxic chemicals. These risks could be mitigated at
further expense by increasing reservoir capacity and improving on early warning
systems; however, such measures could not eliminate these risks entirely.
(b)    Environmental benefits from improved waste water treatment. Daugavpils is one of
the priority "hotspots" under the Baltic Sea Program. Waste water receives only
preliminary and primary treatment, and the reduction of BOD and nutrients is poor.
This has a significant impact on the River Daugava, the Riga Bay and the Baltic Sea.
Under the project, the waste water treatment plant will be rehabilitated and a
biological treatment phase added. This will result in significant reductions in BOD
and nutrients. See Table F-2.
It is assumed that the local benefits of improved waste water treatment (recreational
value from improvement to local beaches and fishing) are captured in the above
estimation of benefits for the water supply component. Similarly, it is assumed that
local benefits of improvements to the sewage pumping stations and gravity sewers
have been captured in the above estimation of benefitis for the water supply
component. The positive externalities associated with improved water quality
downstream of Daugavpils, in Riga Bay and in the Baltic Sea are not easily
quantifiable. A least cost analysis of the alternatives has been conducted including
assessment of feasibility of constructing a new wastewater treatment works at a new
location which would allow increased space needed to allow for longer retention
times. This option was found not to be cost effective. Similarly, use of biofiltration
was examined and was not found to be cost effective.

C. Municipal Development Fund
6.    Projects selected for financing under the Municipal Development Fund must demonstrate a
minimum economic rate of return of 12%. The average is estimated to be 15% but will depend very
much on the distribution of projects among sectors and the extent to which benefits lend themselves to
quanitification.
